     <p><b>Vanderbilt University</b> (or <b>Vandy</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Private_university" title="Private university">private</a> <a href="/wiki/Research_university" title="Research university" class="mw-redirect">research university</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee" title="Nashville, Tennessee">Nashville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, United States. Founded in 1873, the university is named in honor of <a href="/wiki/Ship_transport" title="Ship transport">shipping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail</a> magnate <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Vanderbilt" title="Cornelius Vanderbilt">&quot;Commodore&quot; Cornelius Vanderbilt</a>, who provided the school its initial $1 million endowment despite having never been to the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">Commodore</a> hoped that his gift and the greater work of the university would help to heal the sectional wounds inflicted by the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Today Vanderbilt enrolls approximately 12,000 students from all 50 <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> and over 90 foreign countries in four <a href="/wiki/Undergraduate_education" title="Undergraduate education">undergraduate</a> and six <a href="/wiki/Graduate_education" title="Graduate education" class="mw-redirect">graduate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Professional_school" title="Professional school">professional schools</a>. Several research centers and institutes are affiliated with the university, including the Vanderbilt Institute for Public Policy Studies, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Forum" title="Freedom Forum">Freedom Forum</a> <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_Center" title="First Amendment Center">First Amendment Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dyer_Observatory" title="Dyer Observatory">Dyer Observatory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Medical_Center" title="Vanderbilt University Medical Center">Vanderbilt University Medical Center</a>, the only <a href="/wiki/Level_I_trauma_center" title="Level I trauma center" class="mw-redirect">Level&nbsp;I trauma center</a> in Middle Tennessee. With the exception of the off-campus <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> and satellite medical clinics, all of university's facilities are situated on its 330-acre (1.3&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) campus in the heart of Nashville, only 1.5 miles (2.4&nbsp;km) from downtown. Despite its <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a> surroundings, the campus itself is a national <a href="/wiki/Arboretum" title="Arboretum">arboretum</a> and features over 300 different species of trees and shrubs.</p> 
     <p>Notable alumni and affiliates include two <a href="/wiki/Vice_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Vice Presidents of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Vice Presidents of the United States</a>, 25 <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_Scholars" title="Rhodes Scholars" class="mw-redirect">Rhodes Scholars</a>, seven <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> laureates, <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> winners, and <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> winners.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_early_years">Founding and early years</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding and early years">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg/192px-Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg" width="192" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg/288px-Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg/384px-Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Cornelius_Vanderbilt_Daguerrotype2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Cornelius Vanderbilt
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the years prior to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Episcopal_Church_South" title="Methodist Episcopal Church South" class="mw-redirect">Methodist Episcopal Church South</a> had been considering the creation of a regional university for the training of <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">ministers</a> in a location central to its congregations. Following lobbying by Nashville bishop <a href="/wiki/Holland_Nimmons_McTyeire" title="Holland Nimmons McTyeire">Holland McTyeire</a>, church leaders voted to found &quot;Central University&quot; in Nashville in 1872. However, lack of funds and the ravaged state of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Reconstruction-era&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Reconstruction-era (page does not exist)">Reconstruction-era</a> South delayed the opening of the college.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The following year, McTyeire stayed at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> residence of <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Vanderbilt" title="Cornelius Vanderbilt">Cornelius Vanderbilt</a>, whose second wife was a cousin of McTyeire's wife. Vanderbilt, who was the wealthiest man in the United States at the time, was considering <a href="/wiki/Philanthropy" title="Philanthropy">philanthropy</a> as he was at an advanced age. He had been planning to establish a university on <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">Staten Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)" class="mw-redirect">New York</a>, in honor of his mother. However, McTyeire convinced him to donate $500,000 to endow Central University in order to &quot;contribute to strengthening the ties which should exist between all sections of our common country.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VUhistory_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VUhistory-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The endowment was eventually increased to $1 million (equal to $17,697,692 in 2009<sup id="cite_ref-Inflation_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inflation-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup>) and would be only one of two philanthropic causes financially supported by Vanderbilt. Though he never expressed any desire that the university be named after himself, McTyeire and his fellow trustees rechristened the school in his honor. Vanderbilt died in 1877 without seeing the school named after him.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Renehan_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renehan-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the fall of 1875, about 200 students enrolled at Vanderbilt, and in October the university was dedicated. Bishop McTyeire was named chairman of the Board of Trust for life by Vanderbilt as a stipulation of his endowment. McTyeire named <a href="/wiki/Landon_Garland" title="Landon Garland">Landon Garland</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Mentor" title="Mentor">mentor</a> from <a href="/wiki/Randolph-Macon_College" title="Randolph-Macon College" class="mw-redirect">Randolph-Macon College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> and then-<a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">Chancellor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mississippi" title="University of Mississippi">University of Mississippi</a>, as chancellor. Garland shaped the school's structure and hired the school's faculty, many of whom were renowned scholars in their respective fields.<sup id="cite_ref-VUhistory_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VUhistory-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> However, most of this crop of star faculty left after disputes with Bishop McTyeire.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_with_the_Methodist_Church">Split with the Methodist Church</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Split with the Methodist Church">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Holland_McTyeire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holland_McTyeire.jpg/192px-Holland_McTyeire.jpg" width="192" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holland_McTyeire.jpg/288px-Holland_McTyeire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Holland_McTyeire.jpg/384px-Holland_McTyeire.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Holland_McTyeire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Bishop Holland McTyeire
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During the first 40 years, the Board of Trust, and therefore the university, was under the control of the <a href="/wiki/General_conference_(United_Methodist_Church)" title="General conference (United Methodist Church)">General Conference</a> (the governing body) of the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Episcopal_Church_South" title="Methodist Episcopal Church South" class="mw-redirect">Methodist Episcopal Church South</a>. Tension grew between the university administration and the Conference over the future of the school, particularly over the methods by which members of the Vanderbilt Board of Trust would be chosen, and the extent that non-Methodists could teach at the school.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Conflicts escalated after <a href="/wiki/James_Hampton_Kirkland" title="James Hampton Kirkland">James H. Kirkland</a> was appointed chancellor in 1893. Then the Southern Methodist Church congregations raised just $50,000 in a campaign to raise $300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1905, the Board of Trust voted to limit Methodist representation on the board to just five bishops. Former faculty member and bishop <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Hoss" title="Elijah Hoss" class="mw-redirect">Elijah Hoss</a> led a group attempting to assert Methodist control. In 1910, the board refused to seat three Methodist bishops. The Methodist Church took the issue to court and won at the local level. On March 21, 1914, the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Supreme_Court" title="Tennessee Supreme Court">Tennessee Supreme Court</a> ruled that the Commodore, and not the Methodist Church, was the university's founder and that the board could therefore seat whomever it wished. The General Conference in 1914 voted 151 to 140 to sever its ties with Vanderbilt; it also voted to establish a new university, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Methodist_University" title="Southern Methodist University">Southern Methodist University</a>, and to greatly expand <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1920s_through_World_War_II">1920s through World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1920s through World War II">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Vanderbilt enjoyed early intellectual influence during the 1920s and 1930s when it hosted two partly overlapping groups of scholars who had a large impact on American thought and letters: the <a href="/wiki/Fugitives_(poets)" title="Fugitives (poets)">Fugitives</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Agrarians" title="Southern Agrarians">Agrarians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Carey_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> During the same period, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_William_Goodpasture" title="Ernest William Goodpasture">Ernest William Goodpasture</a> and his colleagues in the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Vanderbilt University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a> invented methods for cultivating <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rickettsiae" title="Rickettsiae" class="mw-redirect">rickettsiae</a> in fertilized chicken eggs. This work made possible the production of <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> against <a href="/wiki/Chicken_pox" title="Chicken pox" class="mw-redirect">chicken pox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_mountain_spotted_fever" title="Rocky mountain spotted fever" class="mw-redirect">Rocky mountain spotted fever</a> and other diseases caused by agents that only propagate in living cells.</p> 
     <p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Vanderbilt was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-list-of-v-12_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-list-of-v-12-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_Rights_movement">Civil Rights movement</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Civil Rights movement">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Oldmech.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Oldmech.jpg/225px-Oldmech.jpg" width="225" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Oldmech.jpg/338px-Oldmech.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Oldmech.jpg/450px-Oldmech.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Oldmech.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Old Mechanical, now part of The Owen Graduate School of Management
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the late 1950s, the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Divinity_School" title="Vanderbilt Divinity School" class="mw-redirect">Vanderbilt Divinity School</a> became a hotbed of the emerging <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a>, and the university <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_(academia)" title="Expulsion (academia)" class="mw-redirect">expelled</a> one of the movement's leaders, <a href="/wiki/James_Lawson_(American_activist)" title="James Lawson (American activist)">James Lawson</a>. In 2005, Lawson was re-hired as a Distinguished University Professor for the 2006–07 academic year, and named a Distinguished Alumnus for his achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university drew national attention in 1966 when it recruited <a href="/wiki/Perry_Wallace" title="Perry Wallace">Perry Wallace</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African-American</a> athlete in the Southeastern Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Wallace, from Nashville, played varsity basketball for Vanderbilt from 1967–1970, and faced considerable opposition from segregationists when playing at other SEC venues. In 2004, a student-led drive to retire Wallace's jersey finally succeeded. <a href="/wiki/Harold_Stirling_Vanderbilt" title="Harold Stirling Vanderbilt">Harold Stirling Vanderbilt</a> was chairman of the Board of Trust between 1955 and 1968 when <a href="/wiki/Racial_integration" title="Racial integration">racial integration</a> was a prominent topic at the school. Today, a statue of him in front of Buttrick Hall <a href="/wiki/Memorial" title="Memorial">memorializes</a> his efforts.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:187px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG/185px-VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG" width="185" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG/278px-VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG/370px-VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:VanderbiltMedEntranceNashville.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The entrance of Vanderbilt Medical School
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1966, the <a href="/wiki/Oberlin_College" title="Oberlin College">Oberlin</a> Graduate School of Theology moved from Ohio to Nashville, and merged with the Vanderbilt Divinity School. In 1979, Vanderbilt absorbed its neighbor, <a href="/wiki/Peabody_College" title="Peabody College">Peabody College</a>.</p> 
     <p>History, race, and civil rights issues came to the forefront on the campus in 2002, when the university decided to rename Confederate Memorial Hall, a residence hall on the Peabody campus as Memorial Hall.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> Nationwide attention resulted, in part due to a lawsuit by the Tennessee chapter of the <a href="/wiki/United_Daughters_of_the_Confederacy" title="United Daughters of the Confederacy">United Daughters of the Confederacy</a>, who had helped pay for the building's construction in 1933 with a $50,000 contribution.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Davidson County Chancery Court dismissed the lawsuit in 2003, but the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Court_of_Appeals" title="Tennessee Court of Appeals">Tennessee Court of Appeals</a> ruled in May 2005 that the university must pay damages based on the present value of the United Daughters of the Confederacy's contribution if the inscription bearing the name &quot;Confederate Memorial Hall&quot; was removed from the building or altered.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The Court of Appeals' decision has been criticized by legal scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In late July 2005, the university announced that although it had officially renamed the building and all university publications and offices will refer to it solely as <i>Memorial Hall</i>, the university would neither appeal the matter further, nor remove the inscription and pay damages.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization_and_administration">Organization and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Organization and administration">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:187px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy_Old_Main.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Vandy_Old_Main.jpg/185px-Vandy_Old_Main.jpg" width="185" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Vandy_Old_Main.jpg/278px-Vandy_Old_Main.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/Vandy_Old_Main.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy_Old_Main.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Old Main (1875), photographed before it burned in 1905
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Vanderbilt University, as a private corporation, is wholly governed by an independent, self-perpetuating Board of Trust. The board comprises 45 regular members (plus any number of trustees emeriti) and the chancellor, the university's <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">chief executive officer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Each trustee serves a five-year term (except for four recently graduated alumni, who serve two two-year terms). <a href="/wiki/Mark_Dalton_(businessman)" title="Mark Dalton (businessman)">Mark Dalton</a> is the board's <a href="/wiki/Chairman" title="Chairman">chairman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_S._Zeppos" title="Nicholas S. Zeppos">Nicholas S. Zeppos</a> currently serves as chancellor of Vanderbilt University. He was appointed interim chancellor after the departure of <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Gee" title="Gordon Gee" class="mw-redirect">Gordon Gee</a>, who left to reassume the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_University" title="Ohio State University">Ohio State University</a> on August 1, 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> and was named chancellor in his own right on March 1, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_history">Administration history</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Administration history">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg/200px-Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg" width="200" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg/300px-Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy-Kirkland-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> After the fire, Old Main was rebuilt with one tower and renamed Kirkland Hall. It is currently home to Vanderbilt's administration.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Since the opening of the university in 1875, only eight individuals have served as chancellor.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Landon Garland was the university's first chancellor, serving from 1875 to 1893. Garland organized the university and hired its first faculty. Garland Hall, an academic building on campus, is named in his honor.</p> 
     <p>The next chancellor was <a href="/wiki/James_Hampton_Kirkland" title="James Hampton Kirkland">James Kirkland</a>—serving from 1893 to 1937, he had the longest tenure of any Vanderbilt chancellor. He was responsible for severing the university's ties with the Methodist Church and relocating the medical school to the main campus. Vanderbilt's Main Building was renamed Kirkland Hall after Kirkland left in 1937.</p> 
     <p>The longest-tenured chancellor was followed by one of the shortest-tenured. <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Carmichael" title="Oliver Carmichael">Oliver Carmichael</a> served Vanderbilt for just nine years, 1937 to 1946. Carmichael developed the graduate school, and established the Joint University Libraries for Vanderbilt, Peabody, and Scarritt College. Carmichael Towers, a set of high-rise dormitories on the northern edge of campus, were named for Chancellor Carmichael.</p> 
     <p>Carmichael's successor was <a href="/wiki/Harvie_Branscomb" title="Harvie Branscomb">Harvie Branscomb</a>. Branscomb presided over a period of major growth and improvement at the university that lasted from 1946 until 1963. He was responsible for opening the admissions policy to all races. Branscomb Quadrangle is a residence hall complex named for the chancellor.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/G._Alexander_Heard" title="G. Alexander Heard">Alexander Heard</a>, for whom the campus's 10-library system (with 3.3 million total volumes) is named, served as chancellor from 1963 to 1982. During his 20-year tenure, the <a href="/wiki/Owen_Graduate_School_of_Management" title="Owen Graduate School of Management">Owen Graduate School of Management</a> was founded, and Vanderbilt's merger with Peabody College was negotiated. He also survived calls for his ouster because of his accommodating stance on desegregation.</p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Joe_B._Wyatt" title="Joe B. Wyatt">Joe B. Wyatt</a> was the chancellor who served immediately after Heard, from 1982 until 2000. Wyatt oversaw a great increase in the university's endowment, an increase in student diversity, and the renovation of many campus buildings. Wyatt placed great emphasis on improving the quality faculty and instruction, and during his tenure Vanderbilt rose to the top 25 in the <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report'</i>s annual rankings for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyatt_Bio_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyatt_Bio-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> The Wyatt Center on Peabody's campus is named for Wyatt and his wife.</p> 
     <p>Gee was appointed chancellor by the Board of Trust in February 2000. Controversy arose in 2006 over Gee's spending during his tenure at Vanderbilt, including the over $6 million spent on remodeling his university-owned house. An article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> in September of that year examining the spending of college and university executives used Gee and Vanderbilt as an example of the lax oversight common to higher education. The Board of Trust has since established a committee to monitor more closely the spending by the chancellor's office.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>After Gordon Gee's departure in 2007, Zeppos was named interim chancellor.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> He was named chancellor <i>suo jure</i> on March 1, 2008, by the university's Board of Trust.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_Center">Medical Center</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Medical Center">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Medical_Center" title="Vanderbilt University Medical Center">Vanderbilt University Medical Center</a> is a vital component of the university and is the only Level I Trauma Center in Middle Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> VUMC comprises the following units:<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Vanderbilt University Hospital, Monroe Carell, Jr., Children's Hospital at Vanderbilt, Vanderbilt-Ingram Cancer Center, Vanderbilt Psychiatric Hospital, Vanderbilt Clinic, Vanderbilt Bill Wilkerson Center, Vanderbilt Stallworth Rehabilitation Hospital, Eskind Biomedical Library, Vanderbilt Sports Medicine, Dayani Human Performance Center, Vanderbilt Page Campbell Heart Institute, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Vanderbilt University School of Medicine">Vanderbilt University School of Medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Nursing" title="Vanderbilt University School of Nursing">Vanderbilt University School of Nursing</a>.</p> 
     <p>With over 21,500 employees (including 2,876 full-time faculty), Vanderbilt is the largest private employer in Middle Tennessee and the second largest in the state (after <a href="/wiki/FedEx" title="FedEx">FedEx</a>, headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>). Approximately 74% of the university's faculty and staff are employed by the Medical Center.<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, the medical center was placed on the Honor Roll of <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report'</i>s annual rating of the nation's best hospitals for the third time, ranking 15th overall in the country, and 17 of the faculty were members of one of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academies" title="United States National Academies">National Academies</a>. In 2004, the university reported that 24.1% of non-Medical Center faculty were women, while 14.4% were of a racial or ethnic minority.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Students_and_faculty">Students and faculty</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Students and faculty">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bensontower.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bensontower.JPG/225px-Bensontower.JPG" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bensontower.JPG/338px-Bensontower.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bensontower.JPG/450px-Bensontower.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bensontower.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Benson Science Hall, one of the first campus buildings, houses the English and history departments.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Profile">Profile</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Profile">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>As of December 2009, Vanderbilt had an enrollment of 6,794 undergraduate and 5,720 graduate and professional students. Students from all 50 states and more than 90 countries attend Vanderbilt, with 60% of the total student body coming from outside the Southeast; 8% of students are from outside the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oua-students_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oua-students-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Moreover, 24% of the undergraduate class of 2010 was non-Caucasian, while roughly half were women.<sup id="cite_ref-profile_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profile-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Vanderbilt offers undergraduates the chance to pursue 70 <a href="/wiki/Academic_major" title="Academic major">majors</a>, or the chance to create their own, in its four undergraduate schools and colleges: the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_College_of_Arts_and_Science" title="Vanderbilt University College of Arts and Science">College of Arts and Science</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Engineering" title="Vanderbilt University School of Engineering">School of Engineering</a>, Peabody College of Education and Human Development, and <a href="/wiki/Blair_School_of_Music" title="Blair School of Music">Blair School of Music</a>. The university also has six graduate and professional schools, including the Divinity School, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Graduate_School" title="Vanderbilt University Graduate School">Graduate School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Law_School" title="Vanderbilt University Law School">Law School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Medicine" title="Vanderbilt University School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_School_of_Nursing" title="Vanderbilt University School of Nursing">School of Nursing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Owen_Graduate_School_of_Management" title="Vanderbilt University Owen Graduate School of Management" class="mw-redirect">Owen Graduate School of Management</a>.</p> 
     <p>The university's undergraduate programs are highly selective: in 2012, the Office of Undergraduate Admissions accepted 14.2% of applicants, thus making Vanderbilt one of the most selective universities in the United States and the most selective university in the state of Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-admissions.vanderbilt.edu_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admissions.vanderbilt.edu-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> In its most recent annual comparison of admissions selectivity, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princeton_Review" title="The Princeton Review">The Princeton Review</a></i> gave Vanderbilt a rating of 99 out of 99.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The admitted first-year class of 2016 had standardized test scores that were well above average: the interquartile range (25th percentile-75th percentile) of <a href="/wiki/SAT" title="SAT">SAT</a> scores was 1400-1560 (Math plus Critical Reading subscores), while the interquartile range of <a href="/wiki/ACT_(examination)" title="ACT (examination)" class="mw-redirect">ACT</a> scores was 32-34.<sup id="cite_ref-profile_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profile-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> For students whose schools reported exact class rankings, 90.4% ranked in the 10% of their class, with an average rank of 2.77%.<sup id="cite_ref-admissions.vanderbilt.edu_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admissions.vanderbilt.edu-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>As with any large research institution, Vanderbilt investigators work in a broad range of disciplines, and the university was among the top 25 recipients of federal research dollars in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2007, Vanderbilt University School of Medicine ranked 10th in terms of NIH funding ($282.3 million). Its <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Space_and_Defense_Electronics" title="Institute for Space and Defense Electronics">Institute for Space and Defense Electronics</a>, housed in the Department of Electrical Engineering and Computer Science, is the largest such academic facility in the world.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG/225px-PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG/338px-PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG/450px-PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PeabodyLibraryVandy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Peabody Library, Vanderbilt University
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Among its more unusual activities, the university has institutes devoted to the study of coffee and of <a href="/wiki/Contract_bridge" title="Contract bridge">bridge</a>. Indeed, the modern form of the latter was developed by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Stirling_Vanderbilt" title="Harold Stirling Vanderbilt">Harold Stirling Vanderbilt</a>, a former president of the university's Board of Trust and a great-grandson of the Commodore. In addition, in mid-2004 it was announced that Vanderbilt's <a href="/wiki/Chemical_biology" title="Chemical biology">chemical biology</a> research may have serendipitously opened the door to the breeding of a <a href="/wiki/Blue_rose" title="Blue rose">blue rose</a>, something that has long been coveted by <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulturalists</a> and rose lovers.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Vanderbilt's research record is blemished, however, by a study university researchers, in conjunction with the Tennessee Department of Health, conducted on <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> during <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> in the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Between 1945 and 1949, over 800 pregnant women were given <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive" class="mw-redirect">radioactive</a> iron. Standards of <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a> for research subjects were not rigorously enforced at that time,<sup class="reference" id="ref_HelsinkiB"><a href="#endnote_HelsinkiB">[B]</a></sup> and many of the women were not informed of the potential risks. The injections were later suspected to have caused cancer in at least three of the children who were born to these mothers.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1998, the university settled a <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action lawsuit</a> with the mothers and surviving children for $10.3 million.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><i>Exploration</i> is the university's online research magazine. It publishes multimedia stories that explain campus research projects ranging from <a href="/wiki/Archeology" title="Archeology" class="mw-redirect">archeology</a> to <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoology</a>, probe the motives of the explorers that perform these studies, and describe the experiences of Vanderbilt students who become involved in scientific research. Vanderbilt undergraduates also publish a journal of original research. Vanderbilt is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_Associated_Universities" title="Oak Ridge Associated Universities">Oak Ridge Associated Universities</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Universities_Space_Research_Association" title="Universities Space Research Association">Universities Space Research Association</a>.</p> 
     <p>Vanderbilt is a member of the SEC Academic Consortium. Now renamed the SECU, the initiative was a collaborative endeavor designed to promote research, scholarship, and achievement among the member universities in the Southeastern Conference. The SECU's goals include highlighting the endeavors and achievements of SEC faculty, students, and universities and advancing the academic reputation of SEC universities.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2013, Vanderbilt University participated in the SEC Symposium in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia" class="mw-redirect">Atlanta, Georgia</a>, which was organized and led by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">University of Georgia</a> and its Bioenergy Systems Research Institute. The topic of the symposium was titled &quot;The Impact of the Southeast in the World's Renewable Energy Future&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings">Rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rankings">edit</a>]</span></h3>
