     <p>In its 2012 edition, <i>U.S. News &amp; World Report</i> ranked Vanderbilt 17th, tied with <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Notre_Dame" title="University of Notre Dame">University of Notre Dame</a>, among all national universities.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Most notably, in the same publication's 2010 and 2011 graduate program rankings, the Peabody College of Education was ranked first in the nation among schools of education. In addition, the Vanderbilt Law School was listed at 16th, the School of Medicine was listed at 15th among research-oriented <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">medical schools</a>, the School of Nursing was listed at 15th, the School of Engineering was listed at 34th, and the Owen Graduate School of Management was listed at 25th among business schools.<sup id="cite_ref-usnwr-grad_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usnwr-grad-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Admissions3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Admissions3.JPG/192px-Admissions3.JPG" width="192" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Admissions3.JPG/288px-Admissions3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Admissions3.JPG/384px-Admissions3.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Admissions3.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Built in 1880 with funding from William Henry Vanderbilt, The Old Gym now houses Admissions.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Additionally, Vanderbilt is ranked first in the nation in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Special_education" title="Special education">special education</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Audiology" title="Audiology">audiology</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> and education administration.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2008, the Owen Graduate School of Management was ranked #30 by <i><a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" title="BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect">BusinessWeek</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-bweekrank_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bweekrank-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Academic_Ranking_of_World_Universities" title="Academic Ranking of World Universities">Academic Ranking of World Universities</a> ranks Vanderbilt as the 50th-best university in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Additionally the ARWU Field rankings in 2012 placed Vanderbilt as one of the top schools in the world in Natural Sciences and Mathematics,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Engineering/Technology/Computer Science,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Life and Agricultural Sciences,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Clinical Medicine and Pharmacy,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> and Social Sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Higher_Education" title="Times Higher Education">Times Higher Education</a></i> 2012, Vanderbilt is ranked 31st in North America and 70th worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2011 <a href="/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings" title="QS World University Rankings">QS World University Rankings</a><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> ranked Vanderbilt university 131st in the world. <a href="/wiki/Human_Resources_%26_Labor_Review" title="Human Resources &amp; Labor Review" class="mw-redirect">Human Resources &amp; Labor Review</a>, a national human competitiveness index &amp; analysis, ranked the university as one of 50 Best World Universities in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> The 2007 <a href="/wiki/Faculty_Scholarly_Productivity_Index" title="Faculty Scholarly Productivity Index">Faculty Scholarly Productivity Index</a>, a measure of the scholarly output of the faculty of nearly 7,300 doctoral programs around the United States, ranked Vanderbilt 8th among large research universities, and 1st in the areas of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_literature" title="Comparative literature">comparative literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Educational_leadership" title="Educational leadership">educational leadership</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, and special education.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Poets_%26_Writers" title="Poets &amp; Writers">Poets &amp; Writers</a> ranked Vanderbilt's English Department's MFA Program in Creative Writing 18th among the top 50 writing programs in the United States in 2010 and 14th in the United States in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine ranked Vanderbilt among the top 100 places to work in the United States, the only university on their list.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus_layout">Campus layout</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Campus layout">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vandyarboretum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vandyarboretum.jpg/225px-Vandyarboretum.jpg" width="225" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vandyarboretum.jpg/338px-Vandyarboretum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Vandyarboretum.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vandyarboretum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Vanderbilt's campus is a designated national arboretum.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The Vanderbilt campus is located approximately 1.5 miles (2.4&nbsp;km) southwest of downtown in the West End neighborhood of midtown Nashville. It has an area of 330 acres (1.3&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>), though this figure includes large tracts of sparsely used land in the southwest part of the main campus, as well as the Medical Center. The historical core of campus encompasses approximately 30 acres (0.1&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>). The Vanderbilt campus is roughly fan-shaped (with the point at the corner of West End and 21st Avenues) and reflects the university's gradual expansion to the south and to the west. The campus is relatively compact, however, and the farthest distance on campus takes about 25 minutes to walk.</p> 
     <p>The oldest part of the Vanderbilt campus is known for its abundance of trees and green space, which stand in contrast to the surrounding cityscape of urban Nashville. The campus was designated as a national arboretum in 1988 by the Association of Botanical Gardens and Arboreta, and over 300 species of trees and shrubs can be found on campus, including one of every species of tree that is indigenous to the state of Tennessee.<sup id="cite_ref-campus_map_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campus_map-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> One tree, the Bicentennial Oak between Rand Hall and Garland Hall, is certified to have lived during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and is the oldest living thing on the campus.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_campus">Main campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Main campus">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vandybicentennialtree.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Vandybicentennialtree.jpg/200px-Vandybicentennialtree.jpg" width="200" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Vandybicentennialtree.jpg/300px-Vandybicentennialtree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Vandybicentennialtree.jpg/400px-Vandybicentennialtree.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vandybicentennialtree.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Bicentennial Oak, facing Buttrick Hall, predates the Revolutionary War.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In the northeast corner of the campus (the base of the fan) is the original campus. The first college buildings, including Kirkland Hall, were erected here in the 1870s, 1880s, and 1890s. This section stretches from West End Avenue south to the Stevenson Center and west from 21st Avenue to Alumni Lawn. The majority of the buildings of the arts and humanities departments of the College of Arts and Science, as well as the facilities of the Law School, Owen Graduate School of Management, and the Divinity School, are located in the original campus. Additionally, the Heard Central Library and Sarratt Student Center/Rand Hall can be found on the original campus.</p> 
     <p>Flanking the original campus to the south are the Stevenson Center for Science and Mathematics and the School of Engineering complex (Jacobs Hall-Featheringill Hall). Housing the Science Library, the School of Engineering, and all the science and math departments of the College of Arts and Science, this complex sits between the original campus and the Medical Center. The Vanderbilt University Medical Center itself takes up the southeastern part of the campus. Besides the various associated hospitals and clinics and the facilities of the Schools of Medicine and Nursing, the medical center also houses many major research facilities.</p> 
     <p>West of the original campus and the Medical Center, Greek Row and the bulk of the Vanderbilt residence halls are found. From north to south, Carmichael Towers, Greek Row, Branscomb Quadrangle, and Highland Quadrangle house the vast majority of on-campus residents in facilities ranging from the double-occupancy shared-bathroom dorms in Branscomb and Towers to the apartments and lodges in Highland Quadrangle. This part of campus is newer than the others; Vanderbilt's westward growth did not start until the 1950s. This portion of campus was built by tearing down small single-family houses and duplexes dating from the early 20th century, and so the area has significantly less green space than the arboretum on the original campus and is more indicative of the university's urban locale.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:239px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Peabodyvu.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Peabodyvu.JPG/237px-Peabodyvu.JPG" width="237" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Peabodyvu.JPG/356px-Peabodyvu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Peabodyvu.JPG/474px-Peabodyvu.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Peabodyvu.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Wyatt Center on Vanderbilt's Peabody campus
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Memorial_Gymnasium_(Vanderbilt_University)" title="Memorial Gymnasium (Vanderbilt University)">Memorial Gymnasium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Stadium" title="Vanderbilt Stadium">Vanderbilt Stadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawkins_Field" title="Hawkins Field">Hawkins Field</a>, McGugin Center, and all the other varsity athletic fields and facilities are to be found in the extreme west of campus. The Student Recreation Center and its associated intramural fields are located south of the varsity facilities.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peabody_campus">Peabody campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Peabody campus">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Directly across 21st Avenue from the Medical Center sits the campus of the Peabody College of Education and Human Development. Due to their separate histories until the merger, the Peabody campus was configured in a radically different style than the original Vanderbilt campus. Whereas the latter has an unplanned organic design with buildings scattered throughout, Peabody campus was planned as a geometric design, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_architecture" title="Jeffersonian architecture">Jeffersonian</a> style of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>. The campus is home not only to Peabody College but also to The Commons, where all freshmen live together as part of the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University#Student_housing" title="Vanderbilt University">College Halls</a> plan. While freshman are now residents of The Commons, it was first inhabited by sophomores before being transformed into a center for first-year living.<br style="clear:both;" /> Peabody campus is also adjacent to <a href="/wiki/University_School_of_Nashville" title="University School of Nashville">University School of Nashville</a> (formerly Peabody Demonstration School.) PDS was part of the school until 1975, and USN now serves as a resource for students of Peabody.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_life">Student life</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Student life">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg/225px-Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg/338px-Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg/450px-Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club_Homecoming_Regatta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Sailing Club Regatta
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizations">Organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Organizations">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university recognizes nearly 400 student organizations, ranging from academic major societies and honoraries to recreational sports clubs, the oldest of which is the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Sailing_Club" title="Vanderbilt Sailing Club">Vanderbilt Sailing Club</a>. There are also more than 50 service organizations on campus, including the Vanderbilt-founded <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_Spring_Break&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alternative Spring Break (page does not exist)">Alternative Spring Break</a>, giving students the opportunity to perform community service across the country and around the world.</p> 
     <p>Despite the lack of an organized journalism curriculum, no less than ten editorially independent media outlets are produced and controlled by students. In addition, a sportswriting scholarship, named for Vanderbilt alumni <a href="/wiki/Fred_Russell" title="Fred Russell">Fred Russell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grantland_Rice" title="Grantland Rice">Grantland Rice</a>, is awarded each year to an entering Vanderbilt freshman who intends to pursue a career in sportswriting. Vanderbilt Student Communications, Inc., (VSC) owns eight print publications, a broadcast radio station, and a closed-circuit television station. One publication, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Vanderbilt_Hustler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The Vanderbilt Hustler (page does not exist)">The Vanderbilt Hustler</a></i>, was established in 1888 and is the oldest continuously published newspaper in Nashville (the newspaper's name references another nickname for the Commodore based on his cutthroat business practices, <i>i.e.</i>, that he &quot;hustled&quot; people out of their money). The on-campus radio station, <a href="/wiki/WRVU" title="WRVU">WRVU</a>, represents the student body by playing a range of music from bluegrass to choral, with a focus on non-mainstream music,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> and students may also have their own talk radio show, while the campus television station, Vanderbilt Television (VTV), showcases student-produced films, skits, and news and entertainment-based shows.</p> 
     <p>VSC was formed as a not-for-profit corporation in 1967 to insulate the university from potential liability and to maintain journalistic independence after a series of controversial articles published by <i>The Hustler</i>. During the 1970s, VSC funded a visiting journalist position to provide advice and counsel to its various operating units. Initially, the directors of VSC included a faculty chairman of the board of directors, several student directors, and an outside journalist director. Among the earlier journalist directors was <a href="/wiki/John_Seigenthaler" title="John Seigenthaler">John Seigenthaler</a>, the then-president, publisher, and editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tennessean" title="The Tennessean">The Tennessean</a></i>, who also played an instrumental role in the creation of <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i>.</p> 
     <p>Additional student publications include those published by the Vanderbilt University Law School, which publishes three <a href="/wiki/Law_review" title="Law review">law reviews</a>; the flagship journal is the <i><a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Law_Review" title="Vanderbilt Law Review">Vanderbilt Law Review</a></i>.</p> 
     <p>Greeks are an active part of the social scene on and off campus, and the university is home to 14 <a href="/wiki/Fraternities" title="Fraternities" class="mw-redirect">fraternities</a> and 10 <a href="/wiki/Sororities" title="Sororities" class="mw-redirect">sororities</a> as of Spring 2011. As of 2006–2007, 35% of men were members of fraternities and 49% of women were members of sororities, or 42% of the total undergraduate population.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2011-2012_nondiscrimination_controversy">2011-2012 nondiscrimination controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 2011-2012 nondiscrimination controversy">edit</a>]</span></h4> 
     <p>In the wake of a <a href="/wiki/Christian_Legal_Society_v._Martinez" title="Christian Legal Society v. Martinez">U.S. Supreme Court decision</a> in 2011, four Christian student organizations were placed on probation due to non-compliance with the university's nondiscrimination policy, which requires student groups to accept all students and forbids them from requiring that their officers share the &quot;beliefs, goals and values&quot; embodied in the group.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Controversy continued to surround this issue throughout 2011 and 2012, culminating in a proposed state law exempting student organizations from nondiscrimination policies. Although the bill passed both houses of the state legislature, Governor Haslam vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG/230px-CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG" width="230" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG/345px-CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG/460px-CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:CorneliusVanderbiltStatue.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Main Campus, looking towards West End Avenue.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honor_Code">Honor Code</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Honor Code">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Since the first classes began at Vanderbilt, the Honor System has served to strengthen the academic integrity of the university. Its principles were outlined in a famous quote by long-time Dean of Students Madison Sarratt:<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote class="templatequote"> 
      <div class="Bug6200">
       Today I am going to give you two examinations, one in trigonometry and one in honesty. I hope you will pass them both, but if you must fail one, let it be trigonometry, for there are many good men in this world today who cannot pass an examination in trigonometry, but there are no good men in the world who cannot pass an examination in honesty.
      </div> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>As a part of their first act together as a class, each Vanderbilt class meets together at the Honor Code Signing Ceremony, where every member of the class pledges their honor and signs the code. The signed pages are then hung in the Sarratt Student Center. The tradition started with the class of 2002. The ceremony is one of only three occasions where a class will be congregated in a single place at the same time (the others being Convocation and Commencement).</p> 
     <p>The Undergraduate Honor Council was formed to help enforce and protect the tradition of the Honor Code. Today, the Honor Council serves two simultaneous aims: to enforce and protect the Honor Code and to inform members of the Vanderbilt community about the Honor System.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_housing">Student housing</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Student housing">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg/275px-Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg" width="275" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg/413px-Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Vandy-Kissam_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Kissam Hall was a men's dormitory from 1901 until it was demolished in 1958. The baths were all in the basement.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Generally, undergraduate students are required to live in dorms on campus. Exceptions are made for students living with relatives in <a href="/wiki/Davidson_County,_Tennessee" title="Davidson County, Tennessee">Davidson County</a>, students with health exemptions, married students, and some students with senior standing. Approximately 83% of undergraduates—freshmen, sophomores, nearly all juniors and most seniors—currently live on campus. The remaining undergraduates join graduate and professional students in living off-campus. Student life at Vanderbilt is consequently heavily intertwined with campus life.</p> 
     <p>However, the on-campus residential system is currently undergoing a radical change. The new system, announced by the administration in 2002, would change the current structure of quadrangle-based residence halls to a new system of residential colleges, to be called &quot;College Halls&quot;. Similar to the residential structures at <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">Rice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a>, the new College Halls system would create residence halls where students and faculty would live together in a self-contained environment, complete with study rooms, cafeterias, laundry facilities, and stores. This project is now underway and is scheduled to be completed within the next 20 years.</p> 
     <p>The first step in the College Halls system is The Commons, a collection of ten residential halls on the Peabody campus that houses all first-year students. It is hoped that The Commons will give first-year students a unified (and unifying) living-learning experience. Vanderbilt renovated five existing residence halls on Peabody and built five new halls to complement them. Two of the new residence halls have received <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> silver certification and the new Commons Dining Center has received gold certification, making Vanderbilt the only university in the state to be recognized by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Green_Building_Council" title="United States Green Building Council" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Green Building Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The university expects all five of the new residence halls and one renovated residence hall to eventually receive LEED recognition.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The total cost of The Commons construction project is expected to be over $150 million.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG/225px-CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG" width="225" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG/338px-CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG/450px-CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:CommonsCVanderbilt.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Commons Center dining hall
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>University administrators believe the undergraduate community receives the greatest benefit from living in on-campus residence halls, citing increased interaction with faculty, better academic performance, and stronger interpersonal relationships.</p> 
     <p>The university broke ground in May 2012 on Kissam College Halls, two colleges that will each house about 330 upperclass students–a mixture of sophomores, juniors and seniors–and be led by faculty directors in residence. Each college will also be divided into two halls led by a graduate fellow. The six existing Kissam Quadrangle buildings, located near the intersection of West End Avenue and 21st Avenue South, will be demolished to make way for the two colleges that will be connected by a shared facility providing gathering space, “grab-and-go” dining options, a classroom, offices and meeting rooms.</p> 
     <p>The $115 million project, a top initiative of Chancellor Nicholas S. Zeppos, is expected to be complete in fall 2014. Funding for the project will be provided entirely through philanthropy and internal resources.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics">Athletics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Athletics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Commodores" title="Vanderbilt Commodores">Vanderbilt Commodores</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:247px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg/245px-Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg" width="245" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg/368px-Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg/490px-Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Memorial_Gymnasium_Vanderbilt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Vanderbilt's basketball teams play in Memorial Gymnasium
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Vanderbilt is a charter member of the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> and is the conference's only private school. The university fields six men's and nine women's intercollegiate teams.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> With fewer than 6,600 undergraduates, the school is also the smallest in the conference; the SEC's next-smallest school, the University of Mississippi, has nearly twice as many undergraduate students. Additionally, the school is a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Lacrosse_Conference" title="American Lacrosse Conference">American Lacrosse Conference</a> for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_lacrosse" title="Women's lacrosse">women's lacrosse</a>, as the SEC does not sponsor that sport. Conversely, Vanderbilt is the only league school not to field teams in <a href="/wiki/Softball" title="Softball">softball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a>, but has discussed adding either or both sports in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Men's and women's <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">tennis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Commodores_men%27s_basketball" title="Vanderbilt Commodores men's basketball">men's</a> and women's <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> are traditionally Vanderbilt's strongest sports. Both teams play in quirky Memorial Gym, built in 1952. Memorial Gym is noted for the location of the team benches on the baselines, the four separate sections of stands on the four sides of the court, the distance of the stands from the court and the elevation of the court above the first few rows of seats on each sideline. The homecourt advantage Vanderbilt has enjoyed has been nicknamed &quot;Memorial Magic&quot;.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> The more recently founded women's <a href="/wiki/Lacrosse" title="Lacrosse">lacrosse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowling" title="Bowling">bowling</a> programs as well as the long-standing men's <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> program are also experiencing moderate national success. After enjoying success in the first half of the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_Commodores_football" title="Vanderbilt Commodores football">football program</a> has generally struggled in more recent times. However, the 2008 Commodore football team finished with its first winning season in over 25 years and with its first <a href="/wiki/2008_Music_City_Bowl" title="2008 Music City Bowl">postseason win</a> in over 50 years.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletics_restructuring">Athletics restructuring</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Athletics restructuring">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hawkins_Field.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Hawkins_Field.JPG/250px-Hawkins_Field.JPG" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Hawkins_Field.JPG/375px-Hawkins_Field.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Hawkins_Field.JPG/500px-Hawkins_Field.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hawkins_Field.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> Hawkins Field in June 2007
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In September 2003, Vanderbilt earned national attention when it announced that it was eliminating its athletic department. Then-Chancellor Gee called Vanderbilt's varsity athletes &quot;isolated&quot;, and insisted that student-athletes would perform better if they were integrated into the rest of the student body. So rather than administer athletics separately from student life, Gee folded the university's varsity teams into the Office of Student Life, the same group that oversees all student organizations. The university is unique in Division I in this regard.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite fears that Vanderbilt would lose coaches and recruits or would be forced out of the SEC, the university has experienced considerable success since the change; 2006–07 was one of the best in the school's athletic history. At one point, seven of Vanderbilt's 16 teams were concurrently ranked in the Top 25 of their respective sports.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Women's bowling won the NCAA championship, bringing the university its first and only team championship since the advent of the NCAA.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The baseball team qualified for the NCAA Super Regionals in 2004, had the nation's top recruiting class in 2005 according to <i><a href="/wiki/Baseball_America" title="Baseball America">Baseball America</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> made the NCAA field again in 2006, and won the 2007 SEC regular-season and tournament championships. Vanderbilt was ranked first in most polls for a large portion of the 2007 season, and the team secured the top seed in the <a href="/wiki/2007_NCAA_Division_I_Baseball_Tournament" title="2007 NCAA Division I Baseball Tournament">2007 NCAA tournament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mascot">Mascot</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Mascot">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>Vanderbilt's intercollegiate athletics teams are nicknamed the Commodores, in honor of the nickname given to Cornelius Vanderbilt, who made his fortune in shipping. Students and alumni refer to Vanderbilt athletic teams as the &quot;Dores&quot; and use several cheers, including &quot;Who ya with?&quot; (usually responded to with &quot;V-U!&quot;), &quot;Anchor Down&quot; and &quot;Go Dores!&quot;</p> 
     <p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">commodore</a></i> was used by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> during the mid-to-late 19th century. A commodore was the commanding officer of a task force of ships, and therefore higher in rank than a captain but lower in rank than an admiral. The rank is still used by the British Royal Navy and other Commonwealth countries, but the equivalent modern-day rank in the U.S. Navy is <a href="/wiki/Rear_admiral_(United_States)" title="Rear admiral (United States)">rear admiral lower half</a>. Since the term was used most during the 19th century, Vanderbilt's mascot, &quot;Mr. C&quot;, is usually portrayed as a naval officer from the late 19th century, complete with <a href="/wiki/Sideburns" title="Sideburns">mutton chops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cutlass" title="Cutlass">cutlass</a>, and uniform.<br style="clear:both;" /></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_faculty_and_alumni">Notable faculty and alumni</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notable faculty and alumni">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Al_Gore,_Vice_President_of_the_United_States,_official_portrait_1994.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg/160px-Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg" width="160" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg/240px-Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg/320px-Al_Gore%2C_Vice_President_of_the_United_States%2C_official_portrait_1994.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Al_Gore,_Vice_President_of_the_United_States,_official_portrait_1994.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Vanderbilt_University_people" title="List of Vanderbilt University people">List of Vanderbilt University people</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Vanderbilt has approximately 114,000 living alumni, with 31 alumni clubs established worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Vanderbilt alumni have gone on to make significant contributions in politics. <a href="/wiki/Lamar_Alexander" title="Lamar Alexander">Lamar Alexander</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">B.A.</a> 1962) is a current U.S. Senator, former <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Tennessee" title="List of Governors of Tennessee" class="mw-redirect">Governor of Tennessee</a>, former <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Education" title="U.S. Secretary of Education" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Secretary of Education</a>, and former U.S. presidential candidate; he filled the Senate seat left vacant by the retirement of <a href="/wiki/Fred_Dalton_Thompson" title="Fred Dalton Thompson" class="mw-redirect">Fred Thompson</a> (J.D. 1971).<sup id="cite_ref-pol-alums_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pol-alums-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Two <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">U.S. vice presidents</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, attended the university, but did not graduate.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Gore's ex-wife, <a href="/wiki/Tipper_Gore" title="Tipper Gore">Tipper</a>, is herself an alumna, receiving a <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degree</a> from Peabody in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Other alumni who are or have been involved in politics include former <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">United States Supreme Court</a> Associate Justice and former <a href="/wiki/U.S._Attorney_General" title="U.S. Attorney General" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Clark_McReynolds" title="James Clark McReynolds">James Clark McReynolds</a> (B.S. 1882), Executive Vice President of the <a href="/wiki/Cato_Institute" title="Cato Institute">Cato Institute</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Boaz" title="David Boaz">David Boaz</a> (B.A. 1975),<sup id="cite_ref-pol-alums_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pol-alums-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> and former <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">White House Chief of Staff</a> <a href="/wiki/John_R._Steelman" title="John R. Steelman">John R. Steelman</a> (M.A. 1924). <a href="/wiki/Bill_Frist" title="Bill Frist">Bill Frist</a>, a cardiothoracic surgeon and former U.S. <a href="/wiki/Senate_Majority_Leader" title="Senate Majority Leader" class="mw-redirect">Senate Majority Leader</a>, is a faculty member at <a href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Medical_Center" title="Vanderbilt University Medical Center">Vanderbilt University Medical Center</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Somalia" title="Prime Minister of Somalia">Prime Minister of Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdiweli_Mohamed_Ali" title="Abdiweli Mohamed Ali">Abdiweli Mohamed Ali</a> (M.A. 1988), is also an alumnus of Peabody College.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg/160px-Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg" width="160" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg/240px-Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg/320px-Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Yunus_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Yunus" title="Muhammad Yunus">Muhammad Yunus</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Within the business world, Vanderbilt alumni have gone on to serve in a number of key leadership roles; alumni include <a href="/wiki/Ann_S._Moore" title="Ann S. Moore">Ann S. Moore</a> (B.A. 1971), former Chairman and CEO of <a href="/wiki/Time,_Inc." title="Time, Inc." class="mw-redirect">Time, Inc.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Doug_Parker" title="Doug Parker">Doug Parker</a> (M.B.A. 1986), President and CEO of <a href="/wiki/US_Airways" title="US Airways">US Airways</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_J._Hart" title="Matthew J. Hart">Matthew J. Hart</a> (B.A. 1974), former President/COO of <a href="/wiki/Hilton_Hotels" title="Hilton Hotels" class="mw-redirect">Hilton Hotels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Vanderbilt also has an intimate connection to the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Management_consulting" title="Management consulting">management consulting</a> industry. In particular, the founders of two of the three most prominent management consulting firms graduated with undergraduate degrees from the university.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Henderson" title="Bruce Henderson">Bruce Henderson</a>, founder of The <a href="/wiki/Boston_Consulting_Group" title="Boston Consulting Group">Boston Consulting Group</a>, graduated with a degree in mechanical engineering in 1937,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Bill_Bain_(consultant)" title="Bill Bain (consultant)">Bill Bain</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Bain_and_Company" title="Bain and Company" class="mw-redirect">Bain and Company</a>, graduated in 1959 with <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa" class="mw-redirect">Phi Beta Kappa</a> honors in history, and currently serves on the Vanderbilt University Board of Trust.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg/180px-Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg" width="180" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg/270px-Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg/360px-Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Jay_Cutler_-_11-01-2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cutler_(American_football)" title="Jay Cutler (American football)">Jay Cutler</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Three alumni, biochemist <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Moore" title="Stanford Moore">Stanford Moore</a> (B.A. 1935), economist and <a href="/wiki/Microcredit" title="Microcredit">microcredit</a> pioneer <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Yunus" title="Muhammad Yunus">Muhammad Yunus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ph.D." title="Ph.D." class="mw-redirect">Ph.D.</a> 1971), and former Vice President Al Gore have won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Four current or former members of the faculty also share that distinction: biochemist <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cohen_(neurologist)" title="Stanley Cohen (neurologist)" class="mw-redirect">Stanley Cohen</a>, physiologist <a href="/wiki/Earl_Sutherland" title="Earl Sutherland" class="mw-redirect">Earl Sutherland</a>, and pioneer molecular biologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Delbr%C3%BCck" title="Max Delbr&uuml;ck">Max Delbr&uuml;ck</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-revu_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revu-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Nobel laureate and neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Greengard" title="Paul Greengard">Paul Greengard</a> was a visiting scholar. <a href="/wiki/Alain_Connes" title="Alain Connes">Alain Connes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaughan_Jones" title="Vaughan Jones">Vaughan Jones</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Fields_Medal" title="Fields Medal">Fields Medalists</a>, are Distinguished Professors of Mathematics at the university. In addition, the university has a rich literary and journalistic legacy. Most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Agrarians" title="Southern Agrarians">Southern Agrarians</a> were a group of influential American writers and poets in the 1920s and 1930s based at Vanderbilt. Three <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate_Consultant_in_Poetry_to_the_Library_of_Congress" title="Poet Laureate Consultant in Poetry to the Library of Congress" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Poets Laureate</a> are Vanderbilt alums: <a href="/wiki/Allen_Tate" title="Allen Tate">Allen Tate</a> (B.A. 1922), <a href="/wiki/Robert_Penn_Warren" title="Robert Penn Warren">Robert Penn Warren</a> (B.A. 1925), and <a href="/wiki/Randall_Jarrell" title="Randall Jarrell">Randall Jarrell</a> (M.A. 1938). Warren later went on to the win the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a>. Novelists <a href="/wiki/James_Dickey" title="James Dickey">James Dickey</a> (B.A. 1949) and <a href="/wiki/James_Patterson" title="James Patterson">James Patterson</a> (M.A. 1970) also graduated from Vanderbilt.<sup id="cite_ref-arts-alums_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arts-alums-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Two well-known sportswriters, Grantland Rice (B.A. 1901) and Fred Russell (B.A. 1927), have a scholarship named after them at the university,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Buster_Olney" title="Buster Olney">Buster Olney</a> (B.A. 1988) writes for <a href="/wiki/ESPN.com" title="ESPN.com">ESPN.com</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-media-alums_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media-alums-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Journalist <a href="/wiki/David_Brinkley" title="David Brinkley">David Brinkley</a> attended briefly.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Skip_Bayless" title="Skip Bayless">Skip Bayless</a> (B.A. 1974) of <i><a href="/wiki/ESPN_First_Take" title="ESPN First Take" class="mw-redirect">ESPN First Take</a></i> attended Vanderbilt as a recipient of the Russell-Rice scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-media-alums_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media-alums-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Geist" title="Willie Geist">Willie Geist</a> (B.A. 1997) is a host of <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Morning_Joe" title="Morning Joe">Morning Joe</a></i>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg/160px-Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg" width="160" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ad/Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Amy_Grant_in_1998.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf9/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Amy_Grant" title="Amy Grant">Amy Grant</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Current <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Bears" title="Chicago Bears">Chicago Bears</a> quarterback <a href="/wiki/Jay_Cutler_(American_football)" title="Jay Cutler (American football)">Jay Cutler</a> (B.S. 2005) is also a Vanderbilt alum and was drafted in the first round of the <a href="/wiki/2006_NFL_Draft" title="2006 NFL Draft">2006 NFL Draft</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Denver_Broncos" title="Denver Broncos">Denver Broncos</a>. Cutler's teammate and offensive tackle <a href="/wiki/Chris_Williams_(offensive_lineman)" title="Chris Williams (offensive lineman)">Chris Williams</a> (B.S. 2008) was a first round pick by the Bears in 2008. Vanderbilt also produced the first overall draft pick of Major League Baseball in 2007 with <a href="/wiki/David_Price_(baseball)" title="David Price (baseball)">David Price</a> and the second overall draft pick of Major League Baseball in 2008 with <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Alvarez_(baseball)" title="Pedro Alvarez (baseball)" class="mw-redirect">Pedro Alvarez</a>. Price and Alvarez were drafted by the then-named <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay_Rays" title="Tampa Bay Rays">Tampa Bay Devil Rays</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a>, respectively. ESPN basketball analyst <a href="/wiki/Carolyn_Peck" title="Carolyn Peck">Carolyn Peck</a> was a standout member of the Commodores' basketball program from 1985–1988, eventually becoming a head coach and leading the <a href="/wiki/Purdue_Boilermakers_women%27s_basketball" title="Purdue Boilermakers women's basketball">Purdue Boilermakers women's basketball</a> team to a national championship in 1999.</p> 
     <p>Given the university's location in Nashville, many of its alumni become involved in the music industry. <a href="/wiki/Dinah_Shore" title="Dinah Shore">Dinah Shore</a> (B.A. 1938), <a href="/wiki/Rosanne_Cash" title="Rosanne Cash">Rosanne Cash</a> (B.A. 1979), <a href="/wiki/Amy_Grant" title="Amy Grant">Amy Grant</a> (B.A. 1982), and <a href="/wiki/Dierks_Bentley" title="Dierks Bentley">Dierks Bentley</a> (B.A. 1997) are all alumni.<sup id="cite_ref-arts-alums_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arts-alums-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Shore later went on to star in a variety of films; other Vanderbilt alumni with Hollywood connections include Academy Award-winners <a href="/wiki/Delbert_Mann" title="Delbert Mann">Delbert Mann</a> (B.A. 1941) and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Schulman" title="Tom Schulman">Tom Schulman</a> (B.A. 1972) and actors <a href="/wiki/Molly_Sims" title="Molly Sims">Molly Sims</a> (B.S. 1995) and <a href="/wiki/Joe_Bob_Briggs" title="Joe Bob Briggs">Joe Bob Briggs</a> (B.A. 1974).<sup id="cite_ref-arts-alums_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arts-alums-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-media-alums_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-media-alums-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanderbilt_University&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a>]</span></h2>
